## 397

B"H, 19 Elul, 5708

To the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing, learned, and distinguished, etc., R' H. H., shlita,

Shalom u'Bracha!

In response to your letter of 4 Elul... I believe I have already written to you that it is worthwhile for you to begin now with whatever hisorerus is possible on your part regarding increasing their share in the distribution of the Vaad HaYeshivos of Chicago. It would be appropriate for you to inform me how much they have received from them on account for the year 5708.

4) As above, please be so kind as to also stir the administration of Yeshivas Tzemach Tzedek - Medrash Shmuel, and they should also inform me of the details of their work—number of students, Roshei Yeshivos, budgets, etc.—if they have not yet done so.

5) Regarding what I wrote—that we do not have the custom of going to the Ohel on the Shabbos before a Yahrtzeit (and this applies to Kabbalas Shabbos in general)—this was to address the opinion that is particular about this custom. But it is obvious that there is no intention to suggest there is any concern in the matter, and in any event, it is no worse than any other Shabbos.

6) As a representative, I am sending you copies of the printed *Kitzurim VeHe'aros LeTanya* by the Tzemach Tzedek, with several additions. I am sure you will confirm their receipt and also endeavor, if possible, to have an appreciative article written about it in an appropriate publication, so that the wellsprings may be spread outward.

7) In continuation of this, perhaps it is finally possible to send here a copy of the glosses of your father, z"l, on the Tanya, which I was told you have in your possession. I am certain they contain explanations from the elder Chassidim as well, and the like.

8) Thank you for the news regarding the observance of my father's, z"l, Yahrtzeit at Yeshivas Toras Emes.

9) Rabbi B. S. Schneerson, shlita, printed an evaluation of the Tzemach Tzedek that was too brief in the newspaper *Beis Yisroel U'Vnei Torah*. Perhaps it would be worthwhile for him to print something a bit more extensive, at least in the *Kol Torah* publication, in accordance with the promise made by Rabbi V. R., shlita, some time ago.

10) One of Anash, shlita, who is currently in the vicinity of Paris, named Rabbi Schneur Zalman Taubal, is requesting that you send him a pair of Rashi Tefillin, of medium size and high quality. Please be so kind as to inform me if this is possible and what the cost would be.

For now, I will conclude with a blessing of *Kesivah V'Chasimah Tovah* for you, your family, shlita, and all those associated with and influenced by you, for a good and sweet year, with revealed and manifest good, in body and soul.

**Rabbi Menachem Schneerson**

## 398

B"H, 19 Elul, 5708

Greetings and blessings!

...Regarding the literature for your daughter, *tichyeh*, they have been and are still being sent to your camp by the office or the branch committee from everything available in our center, and among them is material suitable for the aforementioned. It is understood that I cannot offer an opinion from here on just any books until I see them and examine them.

However, since you have touched upon this question, the main point is not the secondary matter (reading books or even textbooks), although, of course, this is also quite important if it is being neglected (I do not wish to use a more painful expression). The primary matter is the educational institution and the environment there. Even if it is true that for the time being there is no suitable class for her in one of the kosher educational institutions, why do you not endeavor to have such a class established? Have you started and not succeeded? And if it is impossible in any way, why can she not be a student or a pupil in an educational institution that is *al taharas hakodesh*? This is not a question of a class suited to her talents in one or several of the sciences, but a question of an environment suited to her remaining steadfast in her Yiddishkeit. The transition period—both at her age in particular and due to the order of life in our country compared to the order of life in Europe in general—is most relevant to this. Regarding what you write, that this causes you distress, surely you are familiar with the saying of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, *shlita*, in the name of the Rebbe Rashab: "One action is better than a thousand sighs."

I have taken the liberty of writing at some length regarding this... even though I have not done so until now, as you had not yet given me an opening to enter into this matter. In any event, I ask your forgiveness for my intervention in this.

Enclosed is the *Kuntres* for Chai Elul, so that you may use it to bring merit to the many.

I will conclude with the wish of *Kesivah Vachasimah Tovah* for you and your family, *shiy*, for a good and sweet year, *b'gashmius u'b'ruchnius*.

**Rabbi Menachem Schneerson**

#### ***398***

From the secretariat copy.

399

B”H, 20 Elul, 5708

To the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing, learned, and distinguished, etc., Moreinu Harav R’ A. shlita,

Greetings and blessings!

Enclosed is the *kuntres* that the Rebbe, my father-in-law, shlita, has released for publication for 18 Elul. It is certain (*v’vadai*—the acronym for *Vayivarech Dovid es Hashem*, as written in the Siddur of the Arizal) and without doubt (*b’li safek*—the gematria of *Amalek*, which we are commanded to *mocheh timcheh* [blot out]) that you will use it to bring merit to the many.

Regarding these days: The Rambam writes (Hilchos Kiddush HaChodesh, ch. 7, halacha 7) regarding why we do not fix [Rosh Hashanah] on the days of *Adu* (Aleph, Daled, Vav)... that they established a day of fixing and a day (following it) of postponement. However, the Rambam does not explain what sin *Adu* committed that Rosh Hashanah should not fall on them, nor what merit the other days possess, as the Ra’avad questions in his *Hasagos* there (and see the *Peirush HaYad* regarding why the reasons in the Gemara are insufficient).

See the commentary of Rabbeinu Chananel (Shabbos 115a, and see also Sukkah 43b) that according to Rav Hai Gaon, the rule of *Adu Rosh* is entirely a *Halacha L’Moshe MiSinai* (and see also the *Peirush HaYad* there, in the commentary of Rabbeinu Chananel on Rosh Hashanah, and in *She’iltos*, Parshas Vayakhel).

In the *Hagahos* of the Ramaz at the end of *Mavo Shearim*, section 174, he explains this rule according to the path of the *sod* (Kabbalistic mystery).

It may be suggested that the first day of Rosh Hashanah is *dina kashya* (harsh judgment). Therefore, it is postponed from falling on the first day of the seven days of construction, which is the *sefirah* of Chesed, and on the fourth day, which is the *sefirah* of Netzach—both of which are from the right line. And since it is impossible for two days of postponement to be consecutive, there remains for the third day of postponement only the day of Vav. It seems more likely to me that the day of Vav is postponed because of Yesod; the day of Vav is the **sweetening** of the *Gevuros*. Therefore, it is written regarding it "good" (and "very good"—this is *Melech HaMashiach* when it is sweetened) a second time (after man was created; therefore, the first Rosh Hashanah, which began before the creation of man, could have been on the day of Vav, but that is too lengthy to discuss here). This is alluded to in the words of our Sages (Pesachim 54a): Why is "it was good" not said on the second day of the week? Because the light of Gehinom was created on it; [the verse] returned and **included** it on the sixth [day].

See the *Hagahos* of the Mahari A. Chaver there: The second [day] is the harsh judgment, and it is sweetened by the Chesed revealed in the *bris* through the *milah*. This is what is meant by "returned and included it on the sixth."

With this, another point of precision is understood: since the Rambam surely had a reason for the postponement of *Adu* specifically, as the Ra’avad notes, why did he not explain it?

If the reason is as stated above, it is understood. And according to what is brought in *Sefer HaSichos* (Summer 5700, p. 41) in the name of the Baal Shem Tov, the Rambam was a *mekubal* (see my note there regarding this), but he was careful not to reveal it, even by way of allusion, because it was dangerous to do so in his time.

With blessings for a *Kesivah V’Chasimah Tovah* for him and for all those influenced by him, may they live.

**Rabbi Menachem Schneerson**

## T

You are surely occupied with this, and please inform us of the good news regarding the *po'el* and *pe'ulah*. **M. Schneerson**

## 411

411

B”H, 23 Elul, 5708

To the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing, etc.

Greetings and blessings!

Regarding your question on the maamar *Koh Amar Hashem Shimru* 5703, Chapter 4, concerning what is written there—that teshuvah repairs the blemish caused by the failure to fulfill a mitzvah aseh—you ask: Is the intention that the light which was lacking is drawn down, as if the mitzvah aseh had been performed? (In which case, there is a difficulty from Iggeres HaTeshuvah, Chapter 1, which states that the light remains missing). Or is the intention that it is effective only from this point forward?

It is clear from what is written there at the end of Chapter 4 that it draws down not only from this point forward, but also the light that had departed.

As for the difficulty you raised from Iggeres HaTeshuvah, Chapter 1, it is already explained in Likkutei Torah, in the maamar *Mah Tovu*, Chapter 4, that this refers to ordinary teshuvah. However, in the case of profound teshuvah—"From the depths I have called You," etc.—the light that was lacking is also restored.

This can be connected to what is stated in Yoma 86a, that through teshuvah from yirah, one is healed from this point forward, whereas through teshuvah from ahavah, one’s sin is uprooted from its inception.

Signing with blessings for a kesivah v’chasimah tovah,

**M. Schneerson**

Regarding your question on the maamar *Bereishis* 5702—my letters on this subject surely arrived in due time.

## 412

B"H, 25 Elul, 5708

To our esteemed and most honored friend, the G-d-fearing, wise, and good [name], R' Elchanan, may he live, HaKohen.

Peace and blessing!

On the threshold of the coming new year, I express here, in the name of Merkos L'Inyonei Chinuch and in my own name, our heartfelt wishes to you and your honored wife and family, may they live, for a *kesivah v'chasimah tovah* in the Book of Life, blessing, peace, and good livelihood, and all the best in both material and spiritual matters.

Since, in addition to the copies I am sending you, it is appropriate for me to express my thoughts specifically, I would like to take this opportunity, as is my custom, with a *dvar Torah*.

The Rambam, at the end of *Hilchos Shemitah V'Yovel*, explains what the Torah says regarding why a Kohen is not to have a portion in the land like other Jews: because "Hashem is his inheritance." That is to say, because he is occupied with spiritual matters and must fulfill his spiritual duties to the people, the **Aibershter** provides for his livelihood.

The task of a Kohen is "to teach Your ordinances to Yaakov," etc.—to spread Torah and Yiddishkeit among the people. I wish you that in the new year you may be able to carry out all your tasks as a Kohen, and especially in your assistance in the work of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, *shlita*, who is in the category of *Kohen Gadol*.

At the same time, I would like to express our deep gratitude for your sincere interest in and help to Merkos L'Inyonei Chinuch, and I am certain that in the future as well, you will stand by our side to carry out the broad program of Merkos L'Inyonei Chinuch, through which the *kavanah elyonah* is fulfilled.

With greetings and blessing,

**Rabbi Menachem Schneerson**
Chairman of the Executive Committee

## 503

503

B"H, Erev Rosh Hashanah, 5709

To my friend, the Torah-scholar, G-d-fearing, etc., Rabbi Chaim, may he live:

Peace and blessing!

I received your letters in due time, and I ask your forgiveness for the delay in responding, which was due to the many preoccupations that have surrounded me, causing me to prioritize more urgent matters.

Regarding your question on the *maamar* "Ein Omdin" from 5702 (and also from the year 5700), where it is written there "koved rosh" (seriousness), and Rashi explains it as "hachna'ah" (submission) and "shiflus" (humility), and you were troubled by the fact that the word "shiflus" does not appear in Rashi’s actual text.

The same is true in the *siman* of the Maharid at the beginning of *Shemoneh Esrei*.

You should examine *Iggeres HaTeshuvah*, Chapter 10, and the glosses to the *maamar* "Ein Omdin" printed in the *Sefer Piskei HaSiddur* of the Tzemach Tzedek, which cite Rashi’s language **without** the words "and humility."

Therefore, it seems to me that the explanation is that the aforementioned *maamarim* are interpreting the **intent** of Rashi. When Rashi writes "submission," he intends a submission that is associated with humility, to exclude the interpretation of the Bartenura (and see also the *Piskei HaSiddur* there) who interprets it as submission and awe (and in the aforementioned glosses, he explains it according to the Bartenura’s interpretation, as both are words of the living G-d). The distinction is understood according to what is explained further on in the *maamar* "Ein Omdin."

Alternatively, in a slightly different way: "Submission" is both a specific term (submission of the body) and a general term (submission of the body and humility of the soul), similar to the word "Adam," or "Kochav" (which is also a specific name for one of the seven planets), or "Taharos" (a tractate, and also one of the six orders of the Mishnah; see the introduction to the *Piskei HaSiddur* for *Zeraim* regarding this), and others. The aforementioned *maamarim* explain that Rashi chose the term "submission" as a **general** term, and its details are submission and humility.

And even though the *maamar* explains that submission and humility are two separate matters—and if so, how do we know that Rashi’s opinion is that both are required?

It can be said that since the *maamar* concludes that the reason for both (namely, in preparation for prayer) is one (the *kavanah* of the time of prayer), and since there must be submission of the body as a prerequisite for the outpouring of the soul in prayer, then all the more so must there be humility of the soul itself.

See also *Likkutei Torah*, *d"h Lo Hibit Aven*, Chapter 4, regarding the interpretation of our Sages' statement "One should not stand..." that through contemplation with seriousness, "one will come to submission and humility." And there, in *d"h Ra'isi V'hinei Menoras*, Chapter 6: "and to humble and submit the physicality and materiality of the body." In this vein, it is also in the *maamar* *Acharei Hashem Elokeichem* in *Shaarei Teshuvah*, Part 1, and in many other places, though the aforementioned Rashi is not mentioned there.

With this I will conclude with the blessing of *Kesivah V'chasimah Tovah* and a year of peace and redemption,

Your friend, who awaits your well-being,

**Rabbi Menachem Schneerson**

Enclosed is the *Kuntres* for 18 Elul, which was published not long ago.

## 404

404

B"H, Erev Rosh Hashanah, 5709

To the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing, and learned, etc., R' Z. shlita,

Greetings and blessings!

My letters, along with the Kuntres for Chai Elul, surely arrived; please confirm their receipt.

At the end of the year, while reviewing your previous letters, I noticed a question of yours that I have not yet answered, and I ask your forgiveness for the delay.

The question: In Rashi on Micah 6:9, it states: "And wisdom shall see Your name—from here we learn that anyone who says in the *besamim* [at the conclusion of Shabbos] a verse that begins and ends with the same letter as his name, the Torah saves him from Gehinom." You ask what Rashi means by this.

The intent here is the custom brought in several Siddurim—and this is also the Chabad custom—to say in the *besamim* a verse that begins and ends [with the same letter as one's name], as mentioned above. However, we add to the aforementioned Rashi by saying the verse at the end of the *Elokai Netzor* prayer, meaning several times in the *besamim*.

For the time being, I have found this custom*: in *Sefer HaZichronah*, Part 1; in *Siman Avodas Yisrael* for *Bi'ur* (even for a woman!); in *Siman Nehora HaShalem*, and others. But as of now, I have not found it in the *Shelah*, nor at the end of *Kitzur Shelah*, nor in the *Siddur Yaavetz*, even though some cite them.

Regarding the practical application, I asked my father-in-law, the Rebbe, shlita, and he told me that when he studied and began to daven, the Rebbe Rashab—may he be separated between the living and the Source of Life—instructed him to say at the end of davening the verses "Hashem will complete for me..." and "He will bring them out of darkness..." (although there is a sign marked for the name Yitzchak: "Sukkosah..." etc.). And one says this in all prayers and on all days.

It should be noted that it seems there are those who doubt whether the text enclosed in parentheses in Rashi on Nach is actually by Rashi, or by a later author of *minhagim*. The proof is that these customs are not brought by the *Rishonim*, nor in the books of Rashi's students.

I found it written that in the Amsterdam 5460 printing of the Tanach, the glosses of R' Ovadiah were inserted into Rashi, and it is explained there in the introduction that they are by R' Ovadiah HaNavi. I do not have that Tanach at hand to verify. Perhaps the aforementioned [text] in Rashi on Micah is also among these glosses.

I conclude with a blessing for a *Kesivah V'Chasimah Tovah* for you and for the entire group, may they live.

**M. Schneerson**

This letter was delayed until after Rosh Hashanah, and I am enclosing three *maamarim* of *Chassidus*. Please be so kind as to confirm their receipt. They are for your entire group, as agreed upon previously. With *Kesivah V'Chasimah Tovah*.

**M. S.**

From a photocopy of the letter.

## Te

B"H, Elul 5708, to Belinitzky

In response to the questions you asked:

**Question 1.** The contradiction to the understanding of the concept of unity arising from the reality of the worlds—seemingly, this must be resolved, explained, and clarified, in a manner similar to how in the Beis Hamikdash there was a place and yet it was above space simultaneously, for the place of the Aron is not included in the measurements.

**Response.** One must first preface two points:

a) The fact that the place of the Aron is not included in the measurements is not a matter of explanation and clarification, but merely an example and proof of the possibility of the thing—that two opposites can exist at once.

b) Even as an example, the concept of the place of the Aron not being included in the measurements does not fit well here, as you suggest in your question. For we are permitted to say: There is space in reality, there is that which is above space in reality, and both exist simultaneously in reality. On the contrary, this strengthens true faith, for from here is proof that above is the "impossible of impossibilities," as will be explained below. But it is a contradiction in faith to say: "There are worlds in reality, and there is the unity of Hashem in reality, etc."—before explaining that the reality of the worlds is not a matter of duality, Heaven forbid (or at least informing that this is so, even if it cannot be explained by the intellect). And after the explanation of this (that the reality of the worlds is nothing but an illumination, coming only through Tzimtzum, and the Tzimtzum itself, relative to the One who causes the Tzimtzum, is not in reality, and the like), there are no longer two opposites here, and there is no need for the example of space and being above space simultaneously.

Now, in many places in Chassidic discourse, they bring proof from the fact that the place of the Aron is not included in the measurements (and also from the concept of time and being above time simultaneously—when He showed Adam HaRishon generation after generation and its expositors, etc.).

For there are three modes in this matter, and they are analogous to the concept of "that which is," "that which is known," and that which is above both:

1) When one measures the Aron. At that time, he finds two and a half cubits for its length, etc.—no less and no more. And so it is found in the truth of the truth of Torah, which commanded, "And they shall make an Aron... two and a half cubits, etc." And he sees this tangibly. But at the time of this measurement, he knows—though not tangibly—that nevertheless, if he were to measure afterward in the Kodesh Hakodashim, he would find ten cubits in every direction.

This is an example of how there is reality, and yet it is nullified. *Yichud HaTachton* (Lower Unity). However, there are many examples for this, and this is not the point of the concept that the place of the Aron is not included in the measurements. When one measures in the Kodesh Hakodashim and finds ten cubits in every direction—tangibly—and knows that if he were to measure the Aron afterward he would find two and a half, etc., this means he sees tangibly that which is above space, even though he knows it is a matter of space.

This is an example of how space itself is not in reality. *Yichud HaElyon* (Higher Unity) (see *Kuntres Eitz Chaim*, Chapter 2, its summary).

3) When one grasps both matters—the measurement of the Aron and the measurement of ten cubits in every direction—simultaneously, meaning that the reality of both measurements is in an equal manner and force for him.

—And this cannot be said regarding the aforementioned matter of showing Adam HaRishon, for the vision of generation after generation and its expositors was in the eye of the intellect and the power of imagination, i.e., a spiritual eye. The manner and verification of the existence of the time of the generations for Adam HaRishon at the time of his vision is not comparable to the manner and verification of the existence of the time that the vision lasted for him, as is understood.

And this is a decisive proof, for it is the "impossible of impossibilities" being grasped and not grasped simultaneously, for the impossible has no existing nature **above** (and see *Pesach HaShaar* to *Amrei Binah*, Chapter 6).

—And therefore this was specifically regarding the Aron. And therefore this mode does not exist in the example of showing Adam HaRishon.

And this is the point of "the place of the Aron is not included in the measurements."

It is unnecessary to explain that the distinction between the three modes does not depend on what one **wants** to measure first, but rather the intent is—especially in the analogy—that it depends on the qualities and level of the observer and his focus, what is more simple and verified for him, and what is more in a state of renewal and constancy.

Now, in some Chassidic discourses, two of the aforementioned modes are brought together without pointing out the difference between them (for example, *D’H V’Taher Libenu*—of *Chol HaMoed Sukkos*—and *D’H B’Yom HaShemini Atzeres*—of *Simchas Torah* *Ezras Torah*). And the reason is understood: because this discourse does not descend to the details of the matter, but only to its explanation in general (and with this, the contradiction, seemingly, between *D’H Es Hashem He’emarta* 5678 and the aforementioned *V’Taher* regarding the example of the finite and the infinite simultaneously, is also removed).

**Question 2.** Since it is explained that according to the teachings of the Baal Shem Tov, Divine Providence (*Hashgacha Pratis*) extends even to a straw or a leaf—is this specifically in the plant kingdom, but not in the mineral kingdom? And the reason is because many souls are reincarnated in the plant kingdom, which is not the case in the mineral kingdom.

**Response.** By way of preface:

a) Because of the matter of reincarnations, one should not distinguish between the plant and mineral kingdoms, for even in the mineral kingdom there are reincarnations, Heaven forbid, as is written at length in *Shaar HaGilgulim*, Introduction 22, *Shaar HaMitzvos* on *Parshas Eikev*, and elsewhere. And on the other hand, one cannot force Divine Providence in all details of the plant kingdom because souls are reincarnated in them, for it is not necessary that every individual creature of the plant kingdom must have a reincarnated soul.

b) If you were to say that Divine Providence in the inanimate, vegetative, and animate (*domem, tzomeiach, chai*) is specifically because a soul is reincarnated there (or because it is possible that a soul might be reincarnated in them), meaning because this relates specifically to the human species (*medaber*), then there is no innovation here in the Baal Shem Tov’s teaching of Divine Providence, for even those before him admitted to Divine Providence in the *domem, tzomeiach, chai* where it relates to the human being who is being provided for. As is written at the end of the book *Shomer Emunim*, and elsewhere.

But what the Baal Shem Tov revealed to us—and the decisive proof of our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe in this from our Sages regarding the judgment of the pelican and the fish is well known—is that every single detail, even of the *domem, tzomeiach, chai*, is provided for with Divine Providence **from its own perspective**.

And such is also the simple meaning of this statement of our Sages, that they judge the pelican and the fish themselves.

And this matter should be made a detail of the general concept that the Baal Shem Tov explained and publicized, that "Forever, Hashem, Your word stands" in all details of the *domem, tzomeiach, chai, medaber*. Since at every single moment the word of Hashem brings into being every single detail of creation just as it was during the six days of Creation, there is no "free time" for chance. Furthermore, just as all believers do not say there is a matter of chance at the time of creation itself, even in a detail of the *domem, tzomeiach, chai*, so too is it exactly in all the moments of the **existence** of these details, since the Baal Shem Tov made known that they are brought into being at every moment from absolute nothingness.

And more deeply: The entire creation and all the events of the six thousand years that have occurred in the world arose in the primordial thought of *Adam Kadmon*, and all are surveyed there in one glance. It is understood that whatever event it may be, if it had been in a different manner than how it occurred now, there would also have been a change in this glance and thought, as it were. Now, this thought and this general will are according to the Supreme Intent, and they fulfill them precisely—no less and no more. It follows that every event, whatever it may be (and even in the mineral kingdom), in all its details, fulfills the Supreme Intent by the very fact that it occurred in such a manner. —And see *D’H Al Kein Yomru HaMoshlim* 5696 regarding this.

On the contrary, the only type of events that we sometimes do not know if they conform to the Supreme Intent are events of the human species, and specifically the most select and most provided-for among them, namely the Jewish person, for he alone is a possessor of free choice, and it is in his power **to change** his role from what was imposed upon him according to this intent. This is not the case for all other creatures, whose spirit is guarded by His command, may He be blessed, which conforms to the Supreme Intent. See also *Iggeres HaKodesh*, Section 25.

The reason for this in the inner dimension can be said to be because the source of the truth of choice is above the place of the aforementioned intent—"Israel preceded the Torah." See *Likutei Torah*, explanation of *D’H V’Heinif* and *D’H Yom Tov Shel Rosh Hashanah*, beginning.

**Question 3.** If one can use the analogy of the map, found regarding *Ze'er Anpin*, also in explaining the value of the worlds relative to one another.

**Response.** You did not explain to what practical end (*nafka mina*). I mean, every analogy explains several details in the subject being explained. And usually, the details are contradictory, meaning some teach a superiority in the subject, and some—a deficiency. You should have explained to which detail learned from the analogy of the map you are aiming, and asked if it is possible to say it regarding the value between one world and another.

Now, the analogy of the map is in *Bereishis Rabbah* at the beginning—regarding the Torah ("An artisan does not build from his own knowledge, but from blueprints and ledgers, etc.") and see also *Zohar* Vol. 3, 161a. And it is brought in the explanation of the Maggid of "From the beginning... her garments and her covering" regarding *Ze'er Anpin*, which is called Torah, relative to the Essence (and see also *Amrei Binah*, *Shaar Krias Shema*, Chapter 39; *Shaar HaYichud*, Chapter 33, and elsewhere), but it does not descend there to explain the details of the analogy and the differences between it and other analogies.

But in *Shaar HaYichud*, Chapter 12 and onward, the analogy of the map is brought together with three other analogies regarding the *Reshimu* (the "learner in short," the map, the sign, the hint. And what is written there regarding the analogy of the power of the throw—see *D’H B’Yom HaShemini Atzeres*, 5678).

The differences between these analogies were explained in *D’H B’Yom HaShemini Atzeres* 5678, and see also *D’H V’Atah Kadosh* 5657. And the conclusion from there is that in the map, all the details exist (the analogy teaches a superiority in the subject).

And in this—that all the details exist—it is similar to the analogy of the "learner in short," but all this is specifically regarding a foreign object (teaching a deficiency in the subject. And in this—that it is a foreign object—it is similar to the analogy of the sign and the hint).

It is understood that regarding the value of one world to another, one can say the first aspect of the analogy of the map, but not the last aspect, for one cannot say that the world of *Yetzirah* is a foreign object relative to the world of *Beriah*. From this is proof that even regarding the value of the world of *Atzilus* and the world of *Beriah* from their own perspective (and not from the perspective of the light shining within them), one cannot say this.

—And what is written in *D’H Lehavin Inyan HaBrachos*, Chapter 9 (copied in *Hemshech Chayav Adam Levarech* 5638, Chapter 33) regarding the analogy of the map regarding *Al Da'as Tzeir Chaveiro* and *Al Da'as Tzeir Gidulo*, it prefaced there that below on the tablet it is only a likeness and not the essence, but above, etc. See there. Likewise, what is written in the aforementioned *D’H B’Yom HaShemini Atzeres* that the map is appropriate for the world of *Beriah*, etc.—this is regarding the value of the light of the *Reshimu* and not regarding the value of the world of *Atzilus*. See there. —

Now, in *D’H B’Yom HaShemini Atzeres*, it is written that the map is like the matter of an analogy that is regarding a foreign object. And it did not explain the difference between these two. And their difference can be said to be that although in both there are all the details of the subject, the true analogy, which are the analogies of the Torah, is regarding an object that was evolved from the subject (the kingdom of the earth and the kingdom of the heavens, the ten powers of the soul and the *Sefiros*, and the like), which is not the case with the map, which is regarding an object that is completely foreign.

According to this, the example for the value between one world and another is the matter of the analogy and the subject, where it is appropriate in the two aforementioned details, and not the matter of the map.

**Question 4.** If one can say, in explaining the wonder of the Creator from the created, the example of the two letters *Nun-Beis-Ayin* (the letters of the word *Nivra*—created), and there is no way to know the intellect explained in an entire book through knowing two letters from it.

**Response.** Here too, you did not explain your question—to what additional explanation through this analogy, regarding the aforementioned wonder, is your intent.

Now, in the subject, the worlds themselves are the letters of the analogy, which are their source. The knowledge of *Elokus* that comes through "Lift up your eyes on high, etc.," and in general through the worlds and the order of *Hishtalshelus*, and the language of *Eitz Chaim* that the creation was so that they would recognize His greatness, etc.—this knowledge is like the analogy of the intellect clothed in the aforementioned letters in which *Nivra* (the world) was created. And in this there is apprehension, and there needs to be apprehension.

Regarding the wonder of the Creator Himself—this is already explained in *Sefer Shel Beinonim*, Chapter 20, and in many places, the wonder in a more internal way: one word relative to the totality of the power of speech, etc., relative to *Chabad*, etc.

And if you mean by the wonder even from the perspective that He creates worlds, this is discussed in *Sefer Shel Beinonim* there, where it counts a level above speech, but specifically the connection to speech, and as if the root of the root of the worlds is in the Will of the Creator, etc.

Now, it is found in Chassidic discourse (*Likutei Torah*, *D’H Zos Chanukas*, Chapter 2, and elsewhere) the explanation of the wonder from the analogy of two letters, etc., as mentioned above.

For there are three matters of wonder:

a) In *Sefer Shel Beinonim* there, it explains the wonder and the nullification of the creation and the power that brings it into being relative to their root and source above, as mentioned above. For this relates to the knowledge of the concept of unity after *Nivra* (the world was created) just as before *Nivra*.

b) In *Likutei Torah* there, it explains how the fact that *Elokus* brings worlds into being is not at all comparable to the other matters of *Elokus*, even relative to their letters, meaning even relative to their lowest level. And as the known language, "This is not the essence of *Elokus*, that He brings worlds into being, etc." And like the worlds relative to the Torah, which is "longer than the earth, etc." And here is the place of the example about which you asked.

c) Wonder relative to the essence of the soul, not as it is a root and source for speech, also not even as it is connected to revelations higher than speech, but the soul itself—and likewise in the subject above.

And what I wrote above that the wonder relative to the Torah is of the second type, meaning specifically the externality of the Torah, for the internality of the Torah, which is "before Him, etc.," one cannot say even the example of one letter and a complete matter. And as is understood by one who examines the Chassidic discourses on the subject of "David called them songs."

Now, although even in the lowest type there are all three modes of nullification and wonder—(*Memalei Kol Almin*, *Soveiv Kol Almin*, *Kula Kami Ke’Lachash*), the distinction of the three aforementioned types themselves can be said to be analogous to the distinction of *Memalei Kol Almin*, *Soveiv Kol Almin*, and *Kula Kami Ke’Lachash*. And this is not the place to elaborate.
